  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ckiP+F1mVcXZLh2lPmOP/an2lpfMfCBNVX6EBnCMFlfG1Gp6zGxxN5vUpY7Q26wjIeRRV2rjY5OVtTUMrH6x9A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('igTZ/yPRozv5adZpRUhsHdxk1VauGIxo2JL61wRSqoU+mDx9stuHybuTbkKr8E/DVHYSlgiHz7YYWUvd2KANJg==')})
  }

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8889480-how-to-listen-to-and-understand-opera"><img alt="How to Listen to and Understand Opera" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1405364846l/8889480._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_8889480'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8889480-how-to-listen-to-and-understand-opera?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">How to Listen to and Understand Opera<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7283.Robert_Greenberg?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Robert Greenberg<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><\/span> 4.54 avg rating &mdash; 219 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1997\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17188827716598069908\">For more than 400 years, opera has been one of the most popular performing arts. Geniuses—Monteverdi, Mozart, Verdi, Wagner, and Puccini—produced some of the landmark artistic achievements of all time in this form. With Professor Robert Greenberg to <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17188827716598069908\" style=\"display:none\">For more than 400 years, opera has been one of the most popular performing arts. Geniuses—Monteverdi, Mozart, Verdi, Wagner, and Puccini—produced some of the landmark artistic achievements of all time in this form. With Professor Robert Greenberg to show you how, you can learn to understand, appreciate—even to love—opera in just 24 hours of lectures that are a pleasure to hear.\nWith the knowledge of opera from this course, you will understand how music has the power to reveal truths beyond the spoken word; how opera is a unique marriage of words and music in which the whole is far greater than its parts. You will learn the reasons for opera\'s enduring popularity. And you will be able to explore in great depth the extraordinary and compelling world of opera.\n\nProfessor Greenberg is to the lecture what Mozart was to opera. Brilliant, irreverent toward his subject and yet awed by it, he is ingenious in his approach to ensure that his work will have its intended effect on the listener.\n\nThe music is transcendently beautiful. In this course, you will listen to some of the most extraordinary artistic works of all time.\n\nThe history of opera is traced from its beginning in the early 17th century to around 1924. The lectures examine landmark operas; musical, cultural, and social developments that influenced opera\'s growth; and the influence of national languages and cultures on opera.\n\nPart I: The Full Flower and Its Origins\nThe first eight lectures are foundational. You examine the origins of opera and the adaptations of other musical forms that allowed opera to achieve its full effects, first accomplished in Monteverdi\'s Orfeo of 1607.\n\nBut Professor Greenberg does not hide the result while waiting on history to get us there. The course opens with one of the most powerful moments in opera—the dramatically loaded aria &quot;Nessun dorma&quot; (&quot;No one shall sleep&quot;) from Giacomo Puccini\'s Turandot.\n\nIn Turandot, you are exposed to opera\'s unique incorporation of soliloquy, dialogue, scenery, action, and continuous music into an incredibly expressive and exciting whole.\n\nThis famous aria shows us the power of the composer—the power of creating music that goes beyond the words of the libretto to express thoughts and feelings that cannot be expressed in words.\n\nThe study continues with a discussion of how music reveals character and the unconscious state. You are introduced to operatic archetypes such as Figaro and Carmen.\n\nYou examine how the rediscovery of ancient Greek and Roman culture contributed to the riches of the Renaissance. You see the evolution of the madrigal, a form that was ultimately rejected in favor of a more expressive vocal medium: early opera.\n\nPart I of the course concludes with an analysis of the first successful attempt to combine words and music into musical drama, Monteverdi\'s Orfeo of 1607.\n\nPart II: The Aria, the Golden Age, Opera Seria, Opera Buffa\nRecitative, the essence of Monteverdi\'s style, made music subservient to words, but because of its forward-driving nature, recitative cannot express personal reflection.\n\nYou learn how the invention of the aria gave opera composers a powerful tool to stop the dramatic action for characters\' moments of self-reflection.\n\nGluck\'s reforms and his Orfeo ed Euridice of 1762 are addressed as the starting point for the modern opera repertory. The explosion of operas in the Golden Age–Dark Age of opera is discussed. You learn how different voice types are assigned different roles, and how this has varied by culture.\n\nThe rise of opera seria and its characteristics are discussed, along with an analysis of the second act of Mozart\'s Idomeneo—opera seria transcendent.\n\nYou examine the development of opera buffa, from its origins in the popular folklore of the Commedia dell\'Arte to its eventual replacement of opera seria. Mozart\'s brilliant The Marriage of Figaro is discussed as one of the greatest contributions to the opera buffa genre.\n\nPart III: Rossini and Verdi: The Development of French Opera\nYou see how the Italian language and culture gave rise to the bel canto style, with its comic plots, one-dimensional characters, appealing melodies, and florid melodic embellishments.\n\nDr. Greenberg reveals how highly pressurized the business of opera was in the 18th century. Rossini once remarked, &quot;In my time, all the impresarios of Italy were bald by 30.&quot; You are introduced to Rossini\'s The Barber of Seville of 1816 as the quintessential bel canto opera.\n\nYou learn how Giuseppi Verdi broke the bel canto mold. He dominated Italian opera for over half a century by virtue of his lyricism, his emphasis on human emotions and psychological insight, and his use of the orchestra and parlante to drive the dramatic action and maintain musical continuity.\n\nVerdi\'s Otello is discussed as one of the greatest operas of all time.\n\nYou next study French opera and why it became a distinctly different genre from Italian opera. Nineteenth-century French opera—grand opera, opéra comique, and lyric opera—are three distinctive French genres. You\'ll hear why in Act 2 of Bizet\'s dramatically powerful Carmen .\n\nPart IV: Wagner, Strauss, Puccini\nYou see how German singspiel, a play with music, grew from humble origins as a low-class entertainment to high art with Mozart\'s The Rescue from the Harem (1782) and The Magic Flute (1791). You learn how Carl Maria von Weber\'s Der Freischütz established 19th-century German opera.\n\nYou then study Richard Wagner: his personal beliefs, musical theories, and operatic innovations. Wagner turned to the ancient Greek ideal for inspiration, and from it he conceived the idea of an all-encompassing artwork, or music drama, in which the orchestra plays the role of a purveyor of unspoken truths. Dr. Greenberg cites Wagner\'s Tristan und Isolde as the most influential composition of the 19th century, next to Beethoven\'s Ninth Symphony.\n\nRichard Strauss and his controversial opera Salome exemplifies late Romantic German opera.\n\nYou examine Russian opera and nationalism. The late development of Russian opera is outlined from Mikhail Glinka\'s Ruslan and Lyudmila to Modest Mussorgsky\'s Boris Godunov. You see how the Russian language shaped the vocal style of Russian opera.\n\nThe course concludes with an overview of opera verismo, a 19th- and 20th-century genre that favors depictions of the darker side of the human condition; a transcendent example of it is in the pivotal second act of Giacomo Puccini\'s Tosca.\n\nThe essence of opera is debated as you hear part of a scene from Richard Strauss\'s Capriccio. Is it words, or is it music? It is an indefinable combination of both, with the whole greater than the parts.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17188827716598069908\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Her wry account of rural life is undercut, however, by tragedy in its depiction of such troubling events as Matty\'s bankruptcy, the violent death of Captain Brown or the unwitting cruelty of Peter Jenkyns. Written with acute observation, Cranford is by turns affectionate, moving and darkly satirical.\n\nIn her introduction, Patricia Ingham discusses Cranford in relation to Gaskell\'s own past and as a work of irony in the manner of Jane Austen.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">About Charles Dickens</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1387078070p3/239579.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">Charles Dickens
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
23,206
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11200093295648270043"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br/><br/>Dicke</span>
  <span id="freeText11200093295648270043" style="display:none"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br /><br />Dickens left school to work in a factory when his father was incarcerated in a debtors' prison. Despite his lack of formal education, he edited a weekly journal for 20 years, wrote 15 novels, five novellas, hundreds of short stories and non-fiction articles, lectured and performed extensively, was an indefatigable letter writer, and campaigned vigorously for children's rights, education, and other social reforms.<br /><br />Dickens was regarded as the literary colossus of his age. His 1843 novella, <i>A Christmas Carol</i>, remains popular and continues to inspire adaptations in every artistic genre. <i>Oliver Twist</i> and <i>Great Expectations</i> are also frequently adapted, and, like many of his novels, evoke images of early Victorian London. His 1859 novel, <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, set in London and Paris, is his best-known work of historical fiction. Dickens's creative genius has been praised by fellow writers—from Leo Tolstoy to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> and G. K. Chesterton—for its realism, comedy, prose style, unique characterisations, and social criticism. On the other hand, Oscar Wilde, Henry James, and Virginia Woolf complained of a lack of psychological depth, loose writing, and a vein of saccharine sentimentalism. The term Dickensian is used to describe something that is reminiscent of Dickens and his writings, such as poor social conditions or comically repulsive characters.<br /><br />On 8 June 1870, Dickens suffered another stroke at his home after a full day's work on <i>Edwin Drood.</i> He never regained consciousness, and the next day he died at Gad's Hill Place. Contrary to his wish to be buried at Rochester Cathedral "in an inexpensive, unostentatious, and strictly private manner," he was laid to rest in the Poets' Corner of Westminster Abbey. A printed epitaph circulated at the time of the funeral reads: "To the Memory of Charles Dickens (England's most popular author) who died at his residence, Higham, near Rochester, Kent, 9 June 1870, aged 58 years. He was a sympathiser with the poor, the suffering, and the oppressed; and by his death, one of England's greatest writers is lost to the world." His last words were: "On the ground", in response to his sister-in-law Georgina's request that he lie down.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11200093295648270043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/5246858-the-old-curiosity-shop">Quotes from The Old Curiosity...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It is a pleasant world we live in, sir, a very pleasant world. There are bad people in it, Mr. Richard, but if there were no bad people, there would be no good lawyers.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>

            </nobr>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
